B007R3B4RG|4.0|The second and (from the sounds of reports) final solo from Joey Ramone is a solid and unlikely tribute to the late singer. From the liner notes, it also sounds like this was a tough record to release, with barbs at producer Daniel Rey for an &#34;eight year long tug of war&#34; over Joey's vocal tapes. Was the battle worth it? For us fans, heck yeah.<br /><br />&#34;Ya Know&#34; sounds like a half-ways decent Ramones album from the period after &#34;Too Tough To Die&#34; that began seeing them struggling with weaker material. Indeed, some of these songs might have spruced up the likes of &#34;Animal Boy&#34; or &#34;Brain Drain,&#34; as references seem to point to various gestation years. The most blatant is the Elton John cop in &#34;Rock and Roll is The Answer,&#34; the worst is the country weeper redo of &#34;Merry Christmas (I Don't Want to Fight Tonight).&#34; Given that these were all stapled together from home demos, the friends in attendance (Andy Shernoff of the Dictators, Jean Beauvoir of the Plasmatics, Joan Jett, Holly Vincent of Holly and The Italians and more), this sounds like a coherent, full album. Vincent's duet on the Phil Specter-like &#34;Party Line&#34; is a super standout, and &#34;New York City,&#34; which has an all-star cast behind Joey, is a love letter to NYC that rocks like the best of any Ramones platter.<br /><br />Some of the songs retain their demo quality, with La la's and Ba-ba's sounding like open spaces for unfinished lyrics (&#34;Make Me Tremble&#34;) and the closer, &#34;Life's a Gas&#34; is only really half a song. At the same time, when you hear the wind chimes that fade the song out and know that these were things in Joey's life that brought him peace, it's hard not to forgive the album's final handlers for giving us, as fans, everything that was left in the vaults. Almost as good as &#34;Don't Worry About Me&#34; and missing by a cover version (Joey's kicking version of &#34;Wonderful World&#34; on DWAM), &#34;Ya Know&#34; is everything a Ramones fan could expect. A rambling staggering piece of purity. Just like the guy himself.ones fan could expect. A rambling staggering piece of purity. Just like the guy himself.

0345802934|3.0|It's a strange novel... in a bad way, I am afraid.<br />I think Murakami had overdone it this time, overused his special style and techniques. Like his other books, when you read 1Q84, it gives you a feeling that there is a deeper meaning that just escapes you. But when It was over, I realized that probably there was no meaning to begin with.<br /><br />First of all, as all the readers noted, it's very long. But it's not the length really that is disturbing, but the fact that it's unnecessarily long. What is known about a good writing is that it should be written so that every detail and every character is there for a reason. I found that 1Q84 has conversations, situations, characters, etc., that can be easily dropped without changing anything at all, without making any impact. For example, after the first night out with Ayumi, when Aomame and Ayumi have just met, Aomame couldn't remember if her sex partner used condom. This point, the fact that she couldn't remember, was somewhat emphasized. The reader would naturally think that this should mean something, that this situation will have some development. But no - nothing at all. More than that: Aomame is presented throughout as a very cautious person, that does not take unnecessary risk and does not do stupid things...<br /><br />The other flaw is that all characters, including main characters, are undeveloped and flat. Even though sometimes Murakami describes their actions in great details, those details do not contribute really to anything.<br />Ayumi, for example, is so badly presented that she would say things that are entirely out of her character.<br />For example, she, in one scene, makes an almost political speech, when normally all she talks about is sex and other personal matters. People are contradictory creatures, I get it, but I doubt it was the intention of the author to demonstrate that in Auimi's character.<br /><br />The last thing I wanted to mention is that when a book has a number of unexplainedphenomena, it's a good thing, as it allows a reader to wonder and to reach his own conclusion, and therefore, it creates a potential for meaningfulness on different levels. However, when a book consists entirely of unexplained phenomena, it no longer produces the same effect. It, sort of, becomes meaningless. That's what I felt about 1Q84, the meaning of little people, the meaning of \\"irretrievably  lost\\", the meaning of...all the rest. I can think of what all of those things could mean, but they could mean so many things that there is no point really to talk about it.<br /><br />This being said, I give this book 3 stars because:<br />First, the author made me interested enough, so I finished reading.<br />Second, I wonder if by breaking literary rules of 'good literature', it represents a new stream, or a new genre, kind of 'stream of subconsciousness'... In short, I give it a benefit of a doubt; there might be something in it that I failed to discover yet.

B008C1O8CA|5.0|One thing you can say about Green Day in the last ten years is that you never know what you're getting when they release a new album. American Idiot was an ambitious angst ridden rock opera and 21st Century Breakdown was an even more musically ambitious and politically fueled album. Both albums were great and I believe will become classic rock albums with time. Now Green Day has released UNO and are doing something that they haven't done in ten years: they're having fun.<br /><br />  This album is Green Day letting their hair down and having a beer after their two epic projects. The songs are rough and edgy in a good way and have a sarcastic attitude that Billie Joe has resurrected from his earlier albums. The songs do not go as deep as the last two albums, but they instead resonate with the basic daily emotions, anger, and frustration that we feel everyday by using satire and sarcasm. This is a great rock album that does as well in your car CD player as in your stereo at a party. It even has some really great guitar parts. Also, \\"Kill the DJ\\" is worth the price of the album if only to hear a great satire of the pop-synth-rock top 40 bands like Maroon 5 and the like.<br /><br />  Green Day's last 2 albums took us on a wild ride, and their newest album let's us take a breather and kick back with them. A great album and a natural progression in the career of Green Day.

B00008G9JO|5.0|Jethro Tull's eleventh studio album entitled Heavy Horses was released in April of 1978.<br />The album saw the band (which comprised of lead singer/songwriter/flute player Ian Anderson plus guitarist Martin Barre, keyboard player John Evan, drummer Barriemore Barlow, bass player John Glascock whom were now joined by orchestrator David Palmer as second keyboard player) continue in the same direction that its predecessor, 1977's magnificent Songs From The Wood, was which was hard rock mixed with folk and classical influences. At the time of recording, Punk and New Wave were the musical rage and bands like ELP, Yes, Jethro Tull, Genesis, Pink Floyd and their Canadian counterpart Rush and American counterparts like Styx and Kansas were seen as old hat compared to the new bands cropping out of England but Jethro Tull (like Pink Floyd, Rush, Genesis and Styx would subsequently) would see Punk as a kick in the pants to release strong albums (as I did when I heard the album on vinyl late in 2006 and then acquired the remastered CD in 2007).<br />We open the album with the superb ode to the housecat called \\"And The Mouse Police Never Sleeps\\" which is ping-ponging between folk and classic hard rock tinged prog rock. The next track is the folk influenced \\"Acres Wild\\" which is another great song. We follow with the superb hard rocker \\"No Lullaby\\" which spotlights Barlow's drumming and Barre's razor sharp guitar playing. We follow with another folk tinged piece entitled \\"Moths\\" which is another winner of a song. The first side of the album closed with the rocker \\"Journeyman\\" which spotlighted the late John Glascock's superb bass playing and more stellar guitar work from Barre and is a great rocker.<br />The second half of the album starts with \\"Rover\\" which is an excellent prog rocker in the vein of earlier Tull and could be either about a dog or man. \\"One Brown Mouse\\" follows and is another folk influenced number which tells of either a mouse or man and is too a great song. Next is the epic title cut which is the nine minute ode to the plow horses with every influence thrown in (rock, classical, folk and even a fiddle induced romp (which the disco loving sheep didn't get))! The original album ends with the folk rocker \\"Weathercock\\" which is another winner and a great way to end another Jethro Tull album.<br />The Heavy Horses album originally peaked at #19 on the Billboard album chart (which was the band's lowest charting studio album up to that point but disco and soundtracks shanghaied the American music scene) but did manage to sell a million copies nevertheless because the music is just that good!<br />In 2003, the album was re-released as a remastered CD on EMI/Capitol Records with two additional songs. First is \\"Living In These Hard Times\\" which is a great number which first appeared on the Out Of Print 20 Years of Jethro Tull box set. Next is another acoustic piece \\"Broadford Bazaar\\" which first appeared on the 1993 rarities collection Nightcap and is too a great song. It also has all of the original album artwork plus an essay from Ian Anderson himself plus photos, lyrics and thank you credits.<br />Heavy Horses is a great record, and still sounds fresh today.<br />RECOMMENDED!xt is the epic title cut which is the nine minute ode to the plow horses with every influence thrown in (rock, classical, folk and even a fiddle induced romp (which the disco loving sheep didn't get))! B00051E906|4.0|This is worth the asking price, the only reason that it does not get five stars is because the English in the owner's manual is poor. I compared this unit with a state of the art unit used for laboratory and environmental monitoring and it was shockingly dead-on. Very pleased and I fully trust the readings. It will not pick up alpha so several \\"sources\\" will not register. The cost of the included GM tube is about what the whole unit costs and it's not poorly constructed either. I would not drop it or generally toss it around like a phone though. It will operate on one or two AAA batteries which is kinda interesting but the battery life appears to be great. DO NOT put one in backwards as you would with other devices, this will cause them to overheat. They are in PARALLEL and not SERIES meaning if you put one in backwards you have just shorted the + & - together. On a new set of batteries they WILL overheat and leak all over if you do this. The orientation is clearly marked on the battery compartment. Seriously, RTFM if unsure.

B008RV5K4U|4.0|4 stars<br /><br />One of the better DC animated adaptations<br /><br />Based on Frank Miller's classic 4 part mini-series \\"The Dark Knight Returns\\":<br />1. Batman vs. Harvey Dent<br />2. Batman vs. the Mutant gang<br />3. Batman vs. the Joker<br />4. Batman vs. Superman<br />This DVD adapts issues 1 and 2 above.<br /><br />Miller's story from 1986 is one of the two DC miniseries (the other being Watchmen) that unfortunately led comics into the so-called \\"Dark Age\\" of the 90's, when comics had the dark, gritty feel of these books while neglecting to include what actually made these two series great: realistic (well, for comics), adult, intelligent plot-lines, deep characterization, and philosophical/political speculations.  One of the things that made this series (along with Watchmen) unique in its time was that it asked questions like \\"why do super-heroes really do what they do?  Are they really \\"good\\" guys?  Are they, maybe, a little messed-up in the head?\\"  It began the \\"de-construction\\" of super-heroes, and made it impossible to look at them the same naive way again.<br /><br />The story features a Bruce Wayne in his 50's who's been \\"retired\\" as Batman for the past 10 years.  The release of Harvey Dent (previously known as \\"Two-Face\\") from prison, coinciding with an increase in crime in Gotham city (largely involving a huge street gang calling themselves \\"the Mutants\\" (an X-Men reference?)) prompts Batman to come out of retirement.  This inspires a young teenage girl, Carrie, to become a new Robin, who will join Batman in his new war on crime.  Towards the end of the film, you'll see the most intense \\"Batmobile\\" ever seen.<br /><br />The movie does a reasonably good job adapting the story, but it didn't knock my socks off, hence the 4 stars.  I think Peter Weller (Batman/Bruce) and Ariel Winter (Robin/Carrie) did fairly well as the voice actors.  Michael Emerson (Ben from \\"Lost\\") as the Joker only gets a line or two, but I think he'll do a good job in part 2 (to be released Winter of 2013?)  The artwork does a somewhat good job of matching Miller's work (at one point you get the iconic silhoutte with lightning flash from the book's cover), but it's a little too colorful in comparison with the graphic novel (dark and gritty, people!)  The CGI merges somewhat well with the cell animation, but it's a little distracting.  And yes, as other reviewers have mentioned, practically all the \\"interior dialogue\\" (and consequently a lot of the best lines) are gone.<br /><br />Single-Disc DVD extras:<br />-Unskippable (but fast-forwardable) previews for \\"Thunderstruck\\" and a miscelleneous group of DC animated features<br />-Sneak Peek of The Dark Knight Returns, Part 2<br />-Sneak Peek of Superman/Batman Public Enemies: This is an old clip that speaks of the film as if it weren't already available.<br />-Trailers for \\"Thundercats\\" and \\"The Dark Knight Rises\\" and an ad for DC Comics online.<br /><br />Rated PG-13 for violence.  It can be pretty brutal, and I think they had to tone it down a little from the original book to keep it below an R rating.<br /><br />If you're in a hurry to find out what happens next, you can read the original graphic novel.<br />(Note: Let's just not even talk about Miller's sequel \\"The Dark Knight Strikes Back.\\")o be released Winter of 2013?) B004QL7K70|1.0|The thrill of throwing an epic party for your high school friends - and then some - cannot be overstated and is a defining moment of one's adolescence.  As a youth of somewhat reasonable intelligence I always made sure the parties I helped coordinate were at a friend's house - never my own.  I remember quite fondly one such event my junior year I helped put together that almost ended without a single mishap, until one of the last partygoers drunkenly punched a hole through my friend's living room wall as he was stumbling out the door.  Needless to say my friend's parents weren't thrilled when they came home the next day and weren't fooled by the repositioned portrait over the gaping hole.<br /><br />So here we have \\"Project X,\\" a comedy by Nima Nourizadeh of the found-footage variety that reminds us that if you crowd a house with a bunch of unsupervised high school kids a lot of bad things are going to happen.<br /><br />Parties are risky but if done right can do wonders for your social credibility.  Enter Thomas (Thomas Mann), Costa (Oliver Cooper) and J.B (Jonathan Daniel), three friends looking to boost their popularity and score with a bunch of hot chicks.  It's Thomas' birthday and it just so happens to coincide with his parents' anniversary.  When they plan to leave for the weekend allowing Thomas and a few of his friends to enjoy the house, Costa comes up with the brilliant idea to throw the Greatest Party Eva'.<br /><br />If you've seen the trailers or MTV spots you know what happens.  A large gathering quickly spins out of control as more than a thousand people converge on small house vibrating to the beats of the party's DJ.  All of this is shown through the digital lens of a hired cameraman because for some reason they want to capture their criminal exploits - which include buying drugs and getting completely wasted - on camera.<br /><br />Under it all \\"Project X\\" is an ugly, mean spirited film.  The main characters were clearly inspired by the trio from the much superior film - and that's saying a lot - \\"Superbad.\\"  But unlike those social outcasts there is nothing likable about these miscreants.  Thomas could be sensible as the skinny shy kid but only acts for his own self-interests.  Costa impersonates Jonah Hill's Seth from the aforementioned film but without the humor or insightful angst.  He's just mean and gets off on insulting others, especially J.B. for his portliness.  And J.B. is just your nice, overweight geek looking for a little companionship.<br /><br />The movie is overtly misogynistic as most of the female characters are shown exclusively from the neck-down, whether grinding on the dance floor or swimming topless in the pool, and in one example a girl exposes her thong while ascending a staircase in slow-motion.  I hope that cameraman was well-compensated for his voyeurism.  When the film isn't reducing women to body parts, it's showcasing a midget getting stuffed into an oven, a dog being carried away by helium balloons, and anyone with a shred of intelligence unfortunate enough to be near the party getting tazed in the neck.  None of this - and this is the important part - is the least bit funny or entertaining.<br /><br />While this visual abomination is complimented by an excellent soundtrack of pulsing techno and hip-hop, it doesn't stave off the boredom that sets in long before the SWAT team and guy with a flamethrower arrive.  Besides, if I wanted to watch a 90-minute music video I'd waste my time on YouTube instead of this pseudo-porn.<br /><br />The most aggravating thing about \\"Project X\\" is how the action is completely devoid of consequences.  Kids get hammered on booze, slam ecstasy like it's candy, vomit uncontrollably and vandalize property with no payoff.  No one learns anything.  There is no purpose.<br /><br />The last straw for me was how this supposedly unconventional film with its low-budget indie vibe titled \\"Prjoect X\\" relies all-too heavily on the tired cliche of the main character - as flawed an unlikeable as he is - getting the incredibly hot girl.  In this case we have Kirby (Kirby Blanton), one of the few females allowed to show her face and I believe the only female allowed to speak.  She walks in on Thomas as he's in the process of getting laid by another girl in an upstairs bedroom.  She understandably walks out in disgust and promptly ends their relationship.  Thomas realizes how badly he messed things up, so much so that not even his weak speech at the end of the movie will be able to win her back.  Oh wait...<br /><br />I once watched a kid punch a hole through my friend's wall for no reason.  At least we learned something that night and appreciated the experience of throwing party we probably shouldn't have.  The characters in this film learned nothing and became worse for it.  Hopefully that's not the case for all the kids who were inspired by it to throw their own debacles.acter - as flawed an unlikeable as he is - getting the incredibly hot girl. B007GCY8FS|4.0|It's a given that with any series character novels the hero will fall into more bizarre trouble than would any ordinary human in similar circumstance. This is where the willing suspension of disbelief becomes part of the writer/reader contract. Many of these contracts break down over time because of the strain on credulity created by constant complicated plots and seemingly bulletproof heroes.<br /><br />Reacher is different. For while Child carves his hero from the same cloth as other can't scratch `em heroes, he manages to put a real person inside the clothes. Neither likeable nor unlikeable, Reacher's strange and simple rules, and Child's extraordinary ability to weave a complicated, twisting plot/narrative and place interesting, real people inside it is the touchstone of the crime/mystery writer's art.

B008QE9VJI|3.0|Maybe it's because I knew this remake of the old Dan Curtis TV series was \\"Tim Burton on auto-pilot\\" that I dialed my expectations down accordingly. Whatever the case may be, the 2012 \\"Dark Shadows\\" is an imperfect but -- surprisingly -- intermittently charming film that reaffirms how good an actor Johnny Depp can be.<br /><br />The star's performance as Barnabas Collins - a Maine vampire who awakens in the early `70s after having slept for over 200 years, only to find his ancestors on the downturn and his hated rival (a witch played by Eva Green) having overtaken their place in the community - absolutely anchors a sometimes chaotic and under-written (or is it over-written?) Seth Grahame-Smith screenplay that has too many supporting players running around to little or no effect. Yes, Depp's Barnabas is still \\"quirky\\" yet the actor retains enough of his aristocratic origins to make the part not just a rehash of the \\"colorful\\" characters the actor has been essaying of late; he's fascinating, alternately sinister and sympathetic, and the fish-out-of-water material, while not as dominant as the movie's advertising campaign suggested, provides some of the film's more entertaining aspects.<br /><br />The movie does fumble countless dramatic possibilities -- so much of it seems overstuffed and yet undercooked, between Barnabas' weakly drawn relationship with the younger Collins clan (Chloe Grace Moretz's part is close to a throwaway; after prominently establishing her early on, Bella Heathcote's role as the reincarnation of Barnabas' lost love disappears almost completely, resurfacing just in time for the climax), to his confrontation with Green, who turns in an unsatisfying performance (her affected American accent sounds more like she's from Texas as opposed to a witch who's lived in Maine for over two centuries). Michelle Pfeiffer, Jackie Earle Haley, and, of course, Helena Bonham Carter also appear in roles that never seem to serve the dramatic purpose that they shouldhave.<br /><br />It seems evident here that Burton didn't apply himself as much to his craft as usual -- despite using familiar personnel like production designer Rick Henrichs and costume designer Colleen Atwood, the picture has a glossy, \\"digitized\\" appearance unlike his past works -- and even Danny Elfman's score often takes a backseat to a parade of familiar, effectively utilized period pop tunes. Yet there's just something infectious about \\"Dark Shadows\\" when it does score -- particularly in several amusing moments of offbeat humor (Barnabas pounding his head against an electric organ; Alice Cooper showing up to perform at a Collins family party) and through Depp's winning performance. Though still one of Burton's weaker outings all told, it's at least livelier than the moribund \\"Alice in Wonderland\\" and should find a receptive genre audience this Halloween season on video.<br /><br />Warner's Blu-Ray/DVD/Ultraviolet combo pack offers a clear 1080p transfer, with Bruno Delbonnel's cinematography utilizing a striking pallet of colors throughout. The DTS MA soundtrack is effective and extras include just five minutes of deleted scenes (mostly involving Barnabas' relationship with the modern Collins family - material that there should've been more of in the final cut) and \\"Maximum Movie Mode\\" behind-the-scenes \\"focus points.\\"

B008AT71WI|5.0|Alan Menken and Howard Ashman's off-Broadway musical hit was adapted to the screen in 1986 by Ashman (who wrote the script) and director Frank Oz, whose experience with The Muppets made him the ideal candidate to bring the project's offbeat sensibilities to the big screen. Though the film wasn't a box-office smash, Oz and producer David Geffen's screen version of \\"Little Shop of Horrors\\" - itself a remake of the `60s Roger Corman B-movie favorite - ranks as one of the best movie musicals.<br /><br />Rick Moranis stars as the hapless Seymour Krelborn, who works in a flower shop in Skid Row during the early `60s. In love with his co-worker Audrey (Ellen Greene, the only holdover from the stage version), who's a punching bag for her abusive dentist boyfriend (Steve Martin), Seymour's fortunes are stuck in the gutter until he discovers a new plant that feeds on human blood. Dubbed the \\"Audrey II,\\" the little plant sprouts into a full-blown talking - and singing - monster (voiced by Levi Stubbs of the Four Tops) whose aspirations seem to be larger than taking up more and more room in Mushnik's Flower Shop.<br /><br />Menken and Ashman's score is one of their best (and that's saying something): the marvelous early number \\"Downtown,\\" the ballads \\"Somewhere That's Green\\" and \\"Suddenly Seymour,\\" the showstopping \\"Mean Green Mother From Outer Space\\" (written for the film) and the Greek chorus that comments on the action with a Motown-like sound carry viewers through a wacky and wild ride that Oz peppers with occasional star cameos (Bill Murray is hilarious as one of Martin's overly eager patients; John Candy, Jim Belushi and Christopher Guest also briefly appear) and superb puppet effects.<br /><br />When \\"Little Shop\\" was first released in 1986, the movie received mostly positive reviews with an occasional recurring criticism that the film's climactic special effects overshadowed the picture's overall tone. It's ironic this complaint was levied at the movie's theatrical version - one can only imagine the criticism Oz's initial cut of the film would've been met with!<br /><br />Adding nearly 10 minutes of elaborate special effects with the Audrey IIs trashing the planet, the \\"Don't Feed the Plants\\" ending - restored here in Warner's fabulous Blu-Ray release of \\"Little Shop of Horrors\\" - was wisely re-shot after test screenings confirmed that preview audiences almost unanimously hated it. Certainly it does everything some critics complained about the theatrical version and turns it up to the nth degree: shots of the plants eating their way through the streets of New York, tearing down buildings, consuming subway trains, and climbing up the Statue of Liberty are brilliantly designed and executed by Richard Conway and his model unit...but all of it comes out of left field. After watching a fairly intimate and confined musical dominated by two central characters you grow to care about (both of whom are killed off in the original ending), seeing the movie abruptly shift gears and become an FX-driven spectacle along the lines of \\"Godzilla: The Musical\\" simply doesn't work. And while Ashman had intended this ending to be tongue-in-cheek (as it plays in the stage musical), the humor is diminished on-screen because of the scale of the special effects.<br /><br />Though Oz wanted to stay true to Ashman's script and the stage version's story, the dynamics of making a film versus watching a play with live actors lead Oz to admit that he could understand why audiences disliked the original ending. As the director himself states in the Blu-Ray's new featurette, \\"in a stage play you kill the leads and they come out for a bow - in a movie, they don't come out for a bow, they're dead. And the audience loved those people.\\"<br /><br />At least viewers now have the opportunity to see Conway's special effects and Oz's original ending on Blu-Ray - even if, dramatically, it comes up short compared to the smartly reworked finale of the theatrical cut, fans ofthe movie can now see where most of the movie's budget went (buffs will also note that Paul Dooley's appearance has  been restored in the Director's Cut; his part was reshot with Jim Belushi in the revised theatrical cut ending).<br /><br />Warner's Blu-Ray edition of \\"Little Ship of Horrors,\\" which includes both versions of the film, looks as crisp as the film will ever appear: the AVC encoded transfer offers a good amount of detail and lack of DNR while the DTS MA soundtrack is satisfyingly mixed. Extra features are mostly carried over from the original DVD release of the film, the first pressing of which became one of the format's all-time most collectible titles (it offered a B&W version of the unused ending, and then was promptly pulled and repressed without it). Oz's commentary is a carry-over from that release (included on the theatrical version and the original ending of the Director's Cut), as are outtakes/deleted scenes (with Oz's optional commentary), trailers, and a 1986 promo special, \\"A Story of Little Shop of Horrors.\\" Added to this release is a nice, albeit brief, featurette with Oz and Richard Conway discussing the original ending and how thankful they are to Warner Bros. for newly restoring it for home video.<br /><br />Housed in a sleek Digibook with glossy photos, \\"Little Shop\\" fans have a reason to rejoice with a great package no matter which version of the picture they choose to view - though I'm guessing for most viewers, a single viewing of the \\"Director's Cut\\" itself will probably suffice.

B007T99ENE|5.0|Sweet Tooth is Ian McEwan's latest novel and, following the disappointment of \\"Solar\\" marks a return to form. The story of a young woman, who is embarking upon a career in MI5, has the pace of a spy thriller, and is somewhat lighter in touch than many of McEwen's previous novels.  It is written from the point of view of the main protagonist, explores the difficult double life that she is required to lead, but never gets bogged down in overly-long psychological analysis of the complex relationships she is exposed to.  Set in the 1970s the descriptions are accurate and integral to the totality of the work, but never detract from the dynamics of the plot, which is clever and well executed.  It is a pleasure to read the work of an author who is so utterly in command of the English language, and who appears to have a bottomless reservoir of stories waiting to be beautifully told.

055357342X|3.0|I'll preface by saying I only just finished this book and haven't read the rest of the series (or what there is of it so far).<br /><br />Also - Spoilers.  I'll try not to be too specific about it, though.<br /><br />The major complaint seems to be that all the good characters die.  *Several* \\"good\\" characters get the ax, but by no means all or even most of them (depending on who you count under the umbrella of good characters, maybe not even a majority of them).  Conversely, a number of \\"bad\\" characters also get snuffed out, and it seems to me that their deaths will have much greater impact in the future of the series.  What's more significant about the numerous notable character deaths is what it means for the future - with so many prominent people dead, it seems like things in Westeros will necessarily be very, very different from the way they were in the first three novels.  I expect to see major shifts in power, and some formerly small or behind-the-scenes houses rising to prominence.  While this wasn't done in a way that I would have liked (I think he chose kind of a lame way to end the Lannister-Stark war), I do think that all-in-all it was a good move to keep things fresh.<br /><br />I can sympathize to a degree with the sentiment that there's not much worth reading, as at this point in the story the Starks who are many people's favorite group of characters are dwindling into less and less important players in the game.  I enjoy reading about the world and the remaining characters enough to at least give the next book in the series a shot, though.<br /><br />My complaints with the books have more to do with Martin's writing.  I think Martin is a better story teller than he is a writer.  Martin can be painfully repetitive when it comes to already-well-established character details, and I wanted to claw my eyes out after reading that Davos reached for his pouch but it was gone, or that Sandor Clegane hates knights, for the 10th time.  You don't need to tell the reader what they already know, and you *really* don't need to do it 3 times per chapter involving said character.  Character introductions are also redundant, but towards the beginning of the novel he tells the reader who each major character is, as if they hadn't just read 1200 pages in the form of the two preceding novels about these very characters.  Conversely there are so many minor characters that show up pretty infrequently and yet the reader has to fend for himself when identifying them.<br /><br />Martin also seems over-fond of ending chapters on a cliffhanger which is all fine and good from time to time, but doesn't need to happen every. single. chapter.  Additionally, given the format of the books as a series of point-of-view chapters, you're likely to have read through several other characters' perspectives and events before returning to the character whose chapter ended in a cliffhanger, meaning you may well not remember what the cliffhanger was, and it typically turns out to have beeen something mostly insignificant anyways.<br /><br />Between the obsessive attention to well-established detail, redundant character introductions, and the silly cliffhangers his writing can feel very amateurish.<br /><br />In praise, I'll say that the world itself is a lot of fun to read about and immerse yourself in.  His attention to detail gives the reader a very vivid image of the events (which is funny, because for me it's often at odds with the show).<br /><br />I also like that in this book, at least *some* characters begin to feel a little less black-and-white.  Jaime Lannister, Arya Stark, and Sansa Stark all become much more interesting then they had been, and we get a harsh reminder that not *all* of the Starks followers are the honorable and noble Northmen or Riverlanders that they're held up to be.<br /><br />All in all the books are a decent read and I intend to continue with the series, but Game of Thrones and Clash of Kings are both better than Storm ofSwords in my opinion.  We'll see what Feast of Crows brings.

030758836X|4.0|First off: it's hard to write anything lengthy about this book without giving away crucial points, so this is going to be a brief. But I am going to include some info people may consider \\"spoilers,\\" so watch out.<br /><br />I got totally sucked into this book and finished over the course of one plane trip (actually getting snapped at by one flight attendant when I wouldn't put my Kindle away fast enough prior to landing!). You hear books and movies described as \\"roller coaster rides,\\" but this book really is one. It whiplashes you. One minute you think one thing, the next minute you think something else. Then, you don't know what to think. Then you realize that you've been an idiot, and that things are so much more complex than they seem - about the same time the characters in the book realize the same thing.<br /><br />I very much enjoyed reading this book. It is a masterwork. I am not sure how it was possible for Gillian Flynn to craft a story like this and not telegraph plot points, or the ending, which seems to happen so often (where you can say, by page 20, \\"the butler did it\\" or whatever). I have read a lot of mysteries, and a lot of thrillers, but this book honestly kept me guessing the whole way through. I had no idea how it was going to end, which was good. But then the ending came, and that was bad. All I will say is: I think we all have a need, in our reading experiences, to feel like there is some degree of balance or justice that gets meted out to the deserving. Yes, I know that does not happen in real life. Yes, I know the book is better for not having a predictable, good-triumphs-over-evil ending. That all does not make me like the ending any better. Actually, the final scene profoundly disturbed me, because...well, I can't say why without REALLY putting out spoilers. So what I'll say is, I am not sure I would have read the book if I had known how it ended, in advance. But I am glad I did read the book because man, oh man. I have not had a reading experience that riveting or nail-biting in a long time.<br /><br />Like a lot of folks, I look at reviews before I decide to buy books. In this case, I am glad I didn't read the reviews before getting this, because I would have missed out on reading something I really enjoyed. This is one of those rare cases where an ending I found unsatisfying didn't ruin the rest of the book for me. If you're reading these reviews, and you haven't read the book, STOP, read the book, then come back and read the reviews. The reading experience is worth it, even if the ending or other points of the narrative are less-than-satisfying for you.

1594481717|1.0|First for the good points.  There are a few factoids here and there that are pretty interesting but you should be careful because even some of those that appear like factoids are just speculations.<br /><br />Now for the bad.  The author seems to have no understanding of the situation the U.S. is in.  He thinks we went from agriculture, to manufacturing, to information, and now we'll go to a conceptual based economy.  He feels it is progress that we went from a manufacturing based economy to an information based economy, but the real world says otherwise.  The problem is the U.S. doesn't manufacture like it used to and the reason isn't because we've made progress but because the government has levied so many taxes and regulations on manufacturers that they've had to leave the country.  China can't make things cheaper because they're better than we are, it's just that their government doesn't punish them for actually making things.  Take a look at our trade deficit.  We import $40 billion worth of things we can't pay for every month, and we've been doing this for decades.  This can't go on forever and eventually we will have to make things ourselves again instead of relying on other countries to make things for us while we pay for those things with a credit card.<br /><br />With this general concept of right brained thinking and conceptual based work taking over the economy we see serious problems.  The author uses GM as an example, along with their CEO who said he'd use a right brained approach to running the company.  He said \\"what happens to GM happens to the country\\" and he's right, both are bankrupt.  GM is now government motors and they're insolvent without the government purchasing their vehicles.  This is the problem with thinking you can just use \\"conception\\" to create economic growth, you simply can't.  People have to produce things, they have to do so efficiently and effectively and a reasonable price.  GM and many like them can't and that's why they're introuble.<br /><br />The author claims companies should spend more time and money making things have aesthetic qualities but with so many people struggling to make ends meet, most of them aren't going to be willing to pay $10 more for a toaster with a \\"sleek new design\\" when they just need a toaster that works.<br /><br />The author repeatedly says how great it is that we have more and more people graduating with graphic design degrees because these are the types that will revolutionize things.  If that's so then why are these people either unemployed or working as waiters and waitresses?  The author needs to understand basic economic principles and the fact that companies need people who can add value.  They don't need people that learned (or didn't learn) useless skills in college.  These poor students go into severe debt and have no hope of working in their field because their field is not productive for most companies.<br /><br />The bottom line is we need knowledgeable workers that can help produce products, or provide services that are in demand and that doesn't mean spending a ton of money making things \\"pretty\\" when most people can't afford to pay the premium for that prettiness.  We can't have more companies like GM that can't even survive on their own merits.<br /><br />Bottom line, skip this book.  Daniel Pink's book Drive is a good book where he essentially discusses a lot of research.  This book is a series of opinions based on a lack of understanding of economics, business, and marketing.  If you read this book and believe it you'll be in trouble.

B008C1O8CA|3.0|I'll start by saying that I first got into Green Day during the Dookie/Insomniac era, back when I was 15. Over the years, my respect for the band grew with every release as they pushed their skills and musical/lyrical themes. I loved their underrated Warning and was more than pleased with their exuberant American Idiot, which gladly started a new chapter revitalizing their career. Then 21st Century Breakdown came, proving to be another solid record despite the mammoth undertaking of creating a second, culturally-relevant rock opera without coming across as repetitive. So how could they follow up their 2009 three-act opus and make a new name for themselves in the new decade? The answer seems to come in the form of a loosely-knit concept that spans not only 3 acts, but 3 full-length albums. Let me say that when I first heard of the new trilogy I knew that they were capable of pulling it off (and I still believe they have what it takes), although I also suspected that the new records might end up being comprised of what would otherwise have been cast aside as b-sides. Unfortunately--at least with Â¡Uno!--the case seems to be the latter.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong, this is a decent rock album. Billie Joe, Mike and TrÃ© have honed their musical skills over a 25 year career of consistent composing and performing, and the album is a collection of well-crafted tunes. However, they quickly wane under the shadow of Green Day's legacy. They do try new things musically and the album draws influences ranging from Billy Idol to the later days of The Clash (Kill The DJ sounds a lot like The Magnificent Seven and could have been the most refreshing song on the record albeit it suffers due to the lackluster lyrics). However, the problem I see with the record lies in the repetitive nature of many songs (Carpe Diem is disappointingly similar to both Suffocate and Before The Lobotomy at times) and it seems like Billie Joe probably spent less time working in the lyrics department this time around, as for the most part words seem put together with little scrutiny. The F word is gratuitously thrown around in this one, making it lose its intended effect and even turning it into an annoying reiteration (again, Kill The DJ could have been such a great song).<br /><br />I think Green Day would have benefitted from taking a slightly more conservative approach with this new collection of songs. It might have been wiser to put together one solid-as-rock album with the best songs from all 3 records (we've yet to hear Â¡Dos! and Â¡TrÃ©! but so far I'd say one-third of Â¡Uno! would qualify as a-side material) and perhaps release the remaining songs as a second collection of outtakes like they did with Shenanigans in 2002.

B000JMKTLO|4.0|Recently, Gillian Flynn's Gone Girl has gotten a lot of buzz. My husband, a psychologist, and bookaholic friend Jeana, a social worker, have read all of her books and have had good things to say...especially about the mental health status of the characters. Intrigued, I borrowed hubby's e-book of Sharp Objects and gave it a whirl.<br /><br />I won't lie. At first, I had a very difficult time getting into this book. Flynn has a very distinct style of writing with descript, but fragmented and incomplete sentences littered throughout the story. At first, it drove me nuts and I wasn't sure that I even wanted to finish. By 25% in, I was able to acclimate and realized that this stylized narrative was probably a choice made by the author. If so, it was a rather clever one. The main character and our narrator, Camille, is a bit off kilter (to put it mildly) as is the entire story. So the choppy and somewhat abrupt writing helps to draw the reader into her frame of mind.<br /><br />As for characters, Camille is trying to find the light in a loveless life that has been filled with dark desperation. Throughout the book, she constantly battles the urge to carve words into her skin - her primary coping mechanism other than alcohol abuse. When she's feeling especially stressed or down, scars of the words tend to buzz or pulse. This was a super smart and creative way for Flynn to bring the reader into Camille's personal hell. Her mother, Adora, half sister, Amma, and step-father, Alan, are all odd balls with their own well-developed idiosyncrasies that will have you shaking your head in disbelief. Finally, the whole town of Wind Gap seems to have a collective closet full of skeletons, penchant for gossip, and habit of turning a (selective) blind eye. The only semblance of normalcy is the out of town detective from Kansas City, Richard.<br /><br />As I was warned, Gillian Flynn's Sharp Objects was heavy, dark, and most certainly disturbing. It paints an un-pretty and unsettlingly realistic portrait of evil. The plot was carefully crafted with a steady pace that kept me reading, on edge, and hoping I wasn't going to have messed up dreams and nightmares for weeks following the conclusion. Throughout this read, you'll feel exactly how Flynn wants you to - icky and unwell with a serious case of anxiety that you can't quite shake... and it gets progressively worse as you read on. I finished the book considering gender stereotypes of violence, mental illness, cruelty, love, death, murder and so much more. I would absolutely recommend this book to adults who aren't afraid of a dark, sinister psychological thriller. Warning: subject matters include self-mutilation, drug abuse, promiscuity and exploitation of young girls (13 yrs.), abuse, and severe bullying, among others.

B008LA7RM0|4.0|PET SOUNDS has been issued a number of times on CD, initially in 1990, with three mono bonus tracks: &#34;Unreleased Backgrounds&#34; (a snippet of &#34;Don't Talk (Put Your Head On My Shoulder)&#34;); an instrumental called &#34;Trombone Dixie&#34; (both of which later appeared in stereo on [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] box set); and &#34;Hang On To Your Ego&#34; (the original lyric for &#34;I Know There's An Answer&#34;).  Next, several tracks, including an alternate take of &#34;Ego,&#34; were issued on the 1993 box set [[ASIN:B000002UR4 Good Vibrations: Thirty Years Of The Beach Boys]].  After that came the wonderful 1996 (released in 1997) box set [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]], which featured the first-ever true stereo mix of the album, plus instrumental-only and vocal-only tracks (including an early take of &#34;Good Vibrations&#34;), alternate takes, and the remastered original mono album.  The stereo mix (along with the original mono version) was released as an individual CD in both 1999 and 2001; I'm not sure what the differences between the two versions were, except that one of them may have had the original 1990 version of &#34;Ego,&#34; the other the later version from 1993 and 1996, and that the 2001 version had a reconstructed mix of &#34;Wouldn't It Be Nice,&#34; with Mike Love's original mono bridge vocal dubbed into the stereo master.  The 1990 release of &#34;Hang On To Your Ego&#34; had no background vocals, but later releases do. I own the  [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] box set, and in the past had both the 1990 and 2001 CD issues; currently I own the [[ASIN:B000G03Q2S Pet Sounds 40th Anniversary CD+DVD (Limited Edition Fuzzy Package)]], which I believe is essentially the same as the 2001 release, except for the possible restoration of the original &#34;Hang On To Your Ego&#34; (the liner notes state that it is the original 1987 mix (issued in 1990) without background vocals, but the actual track on the CD and DVD contains background vocals), with a bonus DVD containing both a high-resolution stereo and 5.1 surround sound mix of the album (including &#34;Ego,&#34; which is in high-resolution mono), plus three mini-documentaries (including one with the late Sir George Martin), three promotional films, and a photo gallery set to &#34;God Only Knows.&#34;<br /><br />This 2012 reissue is a straightforward reissue of the mono and stereo mixes of the album, without &#34;Ego&#34; or any of the other bonus tracks.  It sounds a little brighter than the 40th Anniversary Edition, or the versions on [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] box set, but is not radically better.  The packaging is also rather skimpy; the digipak has the front cover art with a white banner, the back cover art on the left inside flap, a picture of The Boys from the San Diego Zoo on the right inside flap, and a small picture of the band on the back cover, with the mono and stereo track listings.  The booklet consists of four pages - front cover, back cover, and the track listings on the inside.<br /><br />If you don't have PET SOUNDS, and want the original album without extras, this new version is for you.  Otherwise, I would stick with either the 40th Anniversary Edition or the 2001 version, and I would also recommend either [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] box set or the new [[ASIN:B01DB0EB7S Pet Sounds 50th Anniversary Deluxe Edition]], if you don't own the former (see below).<br /><br />HISTORICAL NOTE: In addition to the single releases &#34;Wouldn't It Be Nice&#34; / &#34;God Only Knows&#34; (Capitol 5706), &#34;Sloop John B.&#34; / &#34;You're So Good To Me&#34; (Capitol 5602), &#34;Good Vibrations&#34; / &#34;Let's Go Away For A While&#34; (Capitol 5676), and the Brian Wilson single &#34;Caroline, No&#34; / &#34;Summer Means New Love&#34; (Capitol 5610), another song from PET SOUNDS was also issued as a single: &#34;Here Today,&#34; issued as the B-side of &#34;Darlin',&#34;  atrack from [[ASIN:B01BMIREJS Wild Honey]] (Capitol 2068). In addition, the stereo version of &#34;I Just Wasn't Made For These Times&#34; was issued as a single in 1996 on the indie Sub Pop label.<br /><br />PET SOUNDS is not the best Beach Boys album - [[ASIN:B008LA7RMK The Beach Boys Today!]] and [[ASIN:B008LA7REI Summer Days (And Summer Nights!!]] get that co-honor - but it is, as the English say, a good 'un, nonetheless.<br /><br />2017 UPDATE: I originally pre-ordered the new [[ASIN:B01DB0EB7S Pet Sounds 50th Anniversary Deluxe Edition]] on Amazon (May 25, 2016), but when I saw the track listing on Universal Music's Web site, I discovered that I already owned about 90 percent of the tracks (save for 11 live versions (recorded at different venues between 1966 and 1993)  and three alternate mixes: a vocal-only version of &#34;I Know There's An Answer,&#34; an alternate remix of the same song, and a new stereo remix of &#34;Good Vibrations&#34; with a partial vocal track) on [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]]. The only other difference is that the mono version in the new collection (found on Disc 1 together with the stereo version, instead of on a separate bonus disc [Disc 4 on the 1997 collection]) is mastered from the [[ASIN:B00FIXN2EU 1972 Brother/Reprise reissue of Pet Sounds]] (Brother/Reprise MS 2197, also issued as a double LP with [[ASIN:B00B9JWZMW Carl And The Passions-So Tough]] [Brother/Reprise 2MS 2083]), supervised by Carl Wilson, instead of the original Capitol master. In addition, four of the &#34;Alternate Versions&#34; on the original box set's third disc (the two promos for the Brian Wilson solo single, &#34;Caroline, No,&#34; &#34;Hang On To Your Ego,&#34; and the aforementioned vocal snippet of &#34;Don't Talk...&#34;) have been moved to the end of Disc 1 on the new collection. Plus, since I didn't own a Blu-ray player then, I could not play the bonus Blu-ray audio disc. Since there was a DVD-Audio disc in the 40th Anniversary Edition,they opted for a Blu-ray version this time around. The DVD also contains video material that is not on the Blu-ray disc (see my comments above). So I cancelled the order at the time.<br /><br />Because I now own a Sony multi-region Blu-ray player, I reversed myself and purchased the 50th Anniversary Edition on July 15, 2017. The Blu-ray audio-only disc contains PCM mono, stereo, and stereo instrumental versions of the album, plus two 5.1 mixes (Dolby True HD and DTS 5.1 Master Audio - not much difference between the two on my sound system). A bonus section for stereo and 5.1 listeners contains six additional tracks: the aforementioned &#34;Unreleased Backgrounds,&#34; the vocal snippet of &#34;Don't Talk...&#34; originally issued in mono on the 1990 CD, but now in stereo and 5.1 on the Blu-ray audio disc (after further review, [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] vocal snippet of &#34;Don't Talk...,&#34; also on Disc 1 of the 50th Anniversary collection, is exactly the same as &#34;Unreleased Backgrounds&#34;), a couple of alternate versions of the &#34;Wouldn't It Be Nice&#34;/&#34;God Only Knows&#34; single, and the beautiful instrumental &#34;Summer Means New Love,&#34; a track originally from [[ASIN:B008LA7REI Summer Days (And Summer Nights!!]], which is included here because it was later issued as the B-side of the aforementioned Brian Wilson solo single &#34;Caroline, No.&#34; The Blu-ray audio sounds great, especially the 5.1 mixes.<br /><br />HISTORICAL NOTE: I haven't listened to the two &#34;Caroline, No&#34; promos in a long time, so when I heard them again recently, I had forgotten that the DJs mentioned were both from the Baltimore/Washington area where I grew up. One promo is for Kerby Scott of Baltimore's WCAO (he also had a teenage dance show on Baltimore's WBAL-TV, and worked in D.C. for a while as well). I don't know what happened to Kerby Scott, but he is probably retired. The other promo is for the late, great Johnny Dark, who worked at WEAM, Arlington, Virginia, at the time of the promo, and also worked at WCAO for many years. On September 13, 1964, Johnny was onstage at the Baltimore Civic Center (now the Royal Farms Arena) introducing The Beatles in their first and only appearance in Charm City (Ringo Starr And His All-Starr Band have returned to Baltimore twice, but Paul McCartney has never been back (you owe Baltimore one, Macca; it's been over half a century)). WCAO switched from Top 40 to country music in the early '80s, and has programmed Black Gospel music since the '90s (as &#34;Heaven 600&#34;). More recently, Dark worked at Washington's WBIG-FM (when it was Oldies 100; it is now classic-rock BIG 100.3, or just BIG 100), and his last gig before his death last fall [2016] was at WTTR-AM 1470, an oldies/classic hits station in Westminster, Maryland. RIP, Johnny - you were to music what the late Hall of Famer Chuck Thompson was to sports broadcasting.<br /><br />Casual Beach Boys fans can probably skip this new release; those who already own [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] should only purchase it if they want the Blu-ray audio disc and the 14 extra tracks not found on the previous set. The  50th Anniversary collection is an essential purchase only for those who don't already own [[ASIN:B000002U4U The Pet Sounds Sessions]] or for hard-core completists.
B008SVFX96|4.0|In my opinion, Richie's two previous solo albums (\\"Stranger In Town\\" and \\"Undiscovered Soul\\") are about as opposite as two albums can be. I think \\"Stranger In Town\\" is a GREAT rock n roll album from start to finish. It was criminally overlooked when it was initially released back in the early 90s. \\"Undiscovered Soul\\" is...not good. I don't know what was going on with that album, but I just could not get into it. At all. So with this album, I had no idea what to expect. I'm happy to report this album is WAY better than \\"Undiscovered Soul\\", but still a step or two behind \\"Stranger In Town\\". In places, it has a signature 80s production sound/feel, while in other places, it sounds very modern (someone in another review mentioned Foo Fighters on the song 'Nowadays'. That's a perfect comparison). Most importantly, the album is a legitimate rock n roll album. Not a metal album, not a modern rock album, not a pop album. Just a straight ahead rock n roll album. There are definitely some hints of the Bon Jovi sound (How could there not be?), but it doesn't sound like a Bon Jovi knockoff. Richie's playing is still top-notch, and lyrically, it covers all the standard emotions a traditional rock n roll album generally covers. If you're looking for a sound that will redefine the future of music, it ain't here. If you're looking for good-time melodic rock, with a few ballads mixed in, this is a solid album. Highly recommended!

0061743526|5.0|I may be the only American under 60 who graduated from high school without having read Harper Lee's \\"To Kill a Mockingbird.\\"  I'd heard about it, of course; I knew there were characters named Atticus Finch and Scout (a girl), and that there was a trial involving racism.  Unlike so many other classics I haven't yet read, however, this one was never \\"spoiled\\" for me, so when I sat down earlier this week to patch up the gap in my reading, I was coming to it as nearly fresh as possible, with only the vaguest idea what I had to look forward to.<br /><br />As the novel opens, the narrator, Jean Louise \\"Scout\\" Finch, professes to tell the reader how her older brother Jem came to break his arm at the elbow years before.  It's a long story, and by the time the unfortunate young man suffers his injury over 250 pages later, not only Jem's arm but the complacency of a small Southern town will be shattered.  The first quarter of the novel offers little hint of the drama that is to unfold; the plucky and precocious Scout, growing up in the early 1930s in Maycomb, Alabama, seems to have only the typical concerns of childhood to ruffle her otherwise tranquil existence: well-meaning but clueless teachers, her brother's occasional bouts of pubescent moodiness, neighbors and relatives who scoff at her tomboyish ways.  Scout enjoys reading with her lawyer father, Atticus; spending time in the kitchen with her family's black cook, Calpurnia; and visiting Miss Maudie, an amiable neighbor.  She, Jem, and their friend Dill, who comes to Maycomb every summer to stay with his aunt, enjoy acting out stories from their favorite books and attempting to coax their enigmatic, reclusive neighbor Boo Radley out of his house.  Everything changes, however, when Atticus is assigned to defend Tom Robinson, a black man accused of raping a white woman, and accepts this assignment with diligence and zeal.  Suddenly Atticus becomes an icon of his town's hopes and a target for its hatreds - and inevitably, his family gets caught up in the mounting storm.<br /><br />\\"To Kill a Mockingbird\\" is simply wonderful, in every way a novel can be.  Scout is a lovable character, whose equal parts innocence and precocity make her the perfect narrator for a story a more mature character might have been too world-weary to tell with such honesty.  Atticus is one of the greatest heroes in fiction, yet there is nothing effortless about his integrity, and he never comes across as self-righteous or too good to be true.  This novel is ethical and wise without ever becoming preachy, suspenseful and dramatic without ever becoming sensational, humorous without becoming silly, realistic without becoming cynical, uplifting without the slightest hint of sentimentality.  In a day when \\"moral ambiguity\\" in literature so often means the absence of any real concept of good or evil, and \\"social justice\\" too often means seeing the world in (sometimes literal) shades of black and white, it's refreshing to see how neatly Lee escapes both pitfalls, populating her novel with characters not merely lovable in spite of their flaws, but basically good at heart, or at least striving for decency, while living in and partaking of a poisonous social order they've simply never thought to question.<br /><br />Books like this are precious and rare.  If you've somehow managed, like me, to make it into adulthood without ever reading it, do yourself a favor and pick it up now.  And if you're still in school, don't wait for it to show up on some mandatory reading list.  Read it, as I did, the way it was meant to be read - for the pure, pure pleasure of it.his family gets caught up in the mounting storm.<br /><br />\\"To Kill a Mockingbird\\" is simply wonderful, in every way a novel can be. The explanation at the front of The Prisoner of Heaven reminds us that it is part of a cycle of novels set in the literary universe of the Cemetery of Forgotten Books of which the previous novels were the first two instalments. It also goes on to say that although each novel is an independent story they are all interconnected by common characters and storylines. The claim is made quite correctly that for this reason the novels can be read in any order, enabling the reader to weave their own path to the heart of the narrative. I do not refute this but personally feel more comfortable with the fact that I have read them in order of publication.<br /><br />The writing is just like in the previous volumes so atmospheric that you get a strong feeling of how Barcelona must have felt in the early nineteen forties and the late nineteen fifties, the two periods we are swapped between in The Prisoner of Heaven. Daniel is now married with a young son and helping his father run the family bookshop, which is struggling to survive. 0061655554|5.0|This was my second time through one of Wall Street's most amazing stories. It's depiction of the Leveraged Buyout craze of the 1980's is a cautionary tale of greed that remains unheeded. An LBO is a financial transaction and process by which \\"a small group of senior executives, usually working with a Wall Street partner, proposes to buy its company from public shareholders, using massive amounts of borrowed money\\". Colorful Ross Johnson from my hometown of Winnipeg headed RJR Nabisco and recklessly sought to unlock the value he believed investors were overlooking in the company. This organization was the 19th largest company at the time with 140,000 employees and a host of impressive brands: Oreos, Ritz crackers, Life Savers, and Winston and Salem cigarettes. The eventual transaction ended up being the largest LBO ever reaching $17 billion in value (and huge heights of controversy).<br /><br />Johnson could be a fictional invention. His rise in corporate power was built on political manoeuvring, extensive expense accounts, fleets of corporate jets (RJR Nabsico had 36 pilots and 10 planes), hobnobbing, and other excesses that make for entertaining (and these days incomprehensible) reading. His desire to eschew corporate tradition and civility is at odds with a traditional, depression upbringing. But Ross was his own man and showed early signs of being an entrepreneur and dealmaker. He was also big on the social scene being president of his fraternity, varsity basketball player, and a Cadet in Canadian military officer training at the University of Manitoba. The book is replete with examples of Ross' love of partying and the good life. At one time he was a member of over twenty country clubs and always seemed able to hit the links no matter what was going on.<br /><br />The book reintroduces us to the firms and players that would personify a time when financial black magic took precedent over business basics like valuable products and solid customer service (not much has changed). In it are: Henry Kravis of Kohlberg Kravis; Salomon Brothers; Jim and Linda Robinson; Lazard Freres & Co.; Jeffrey \\"Mad Dog\\" Beck; Morgan Stanley; Drexel Burnham Lambert; Forstmann Little; Goldman Sachs; Shearson Lehman; and more. The majority of the book covers the feeding frenzy that ensued once the company was in play. Greed, petty jealousies, tantrums, egos, arrogance and ignorance are all in great supply. I enjoyed the side stories covering Ted Forstmann who hated Kohlberg Kravis and John Greeniaus who was one executive capable of actual management.<br /><br />Burrough and Helyar place the reader in the boardroom, limo, and bar. The book is so well researched, the narrative so engaging, and the pace so lively that it reads like a novel. It continues to influence financial and business reporting by placing emphasis on the very real human foibles that impact those worlds. It made me a bit of a junkie for similar works as I went on to read others of this genre and era including: Den of Thieves, Predator's Ball, Rainmaker, amongst others.has changed). In it are: Henry Kravis of Kohlberg Kravis; Salomon Brothers; Jim and Linda Robinson; Lazard Freres & Co.; Jeffrey \\"Mad Dog\\" Beck; Morgan Stanley; Drexel Burnham Lambert; Forstmann Little; Goldman Sachs; Shearson Lehman; and more. The majority of the book covers the feeding frenzy that ensued once the company was in play. Greed, petty jealousies, tantrums, egos, arrogance and ignorance are all in great supply. It all just made me wince with how idiotic she sounded. By her own account, she went to college. She was too silly to pull it together long enough to actually graduate but she did matriculate somewhere. All I can think is that she actually just wanted the trail to kill her so she could be put out of her misery.<br /><br />Finally we have the complete non-sequiturs in her writing. She still manages to find drugs.<br /><br />This book makes me feel embarrassed for women everywhere. 0871404133|3.0|E. O. Wilson, the esteemed Harvard ant specialist and world-class biologist, is no stranger to controversy, having survived the so-called \\"sociobiology wars\\" of the 1970s. Unlike most biologists, who are content to put out a few peer-reviewed publications in high-impact scientific journals as a means of advancing through the ranks of academia (assistant, associate, and full professor), Dr. Wilson has done the unusual and invented whole new fields of biological endeavor, which in turn have given issue to other areas of inquiry and otherwise informed other established fields. It is no different in E. O. Wilson, the esteemed Harvard ant specialist and world-class biologist, is no stranger to controversy, having survived the so-called \\"sociobiology wars\\" of the 1970s. Unlike most biologists, who are content to put out a few peer-reviewed publications in high-impact scientific journals as a means of advancing through the ranks of academia (assistant, associate, and full professor), Dr. Wilson has done the unusual and invented whole new fields of biological endeavor, which in turn have given issue to other areas of inquiry and otherwise informed other established fields. It is no different in \\"The Social Conquest of Earth,\\" in which Wilson sets out to provide the long-sought answers to the long-standing questions about the human condition: \\"Where do we come from?\\" \\"What are we?\\" \\"Where are we going?\\" Cleverly, Wilson initiates his search for these answers by introducing us to a painting many of us have seen, and whose artist we can identify, but perhaps not many of us can name. The painting is Paul Gauguin's famous \\"D'oÃ¹ venons nous? Que sommes nous? OÃ¹ allons nous?\\" Three of the main sections of Wilson's book are given over to answering these questions, of course, through the lens of an evolutionary biologist who has spent much of his career studying eusocial insects and, reflectively, the eusocial human species. He has searched for answers to these questions previously in a series of masterful books, for two of which he has won Pulitzer Prizes. In addition, he has sought to remind us that of all the sources of wealth available to humans, biological wealth is of the greatest value, making biodiversity decline the most horrendous of crimes humans visit upon themselves and the planet at large. In a sense, Wilson has been preadapted (to use a word frequently used by the author) to explore such fundamental questions about the human condition, since he is a myrmecologist, a student of ants. Ants represent the other pinnacle of eusociality, which, by most any measure of biological success, have done much more with this capacity than have humans. So, one of the most important questions he and his fellow myrmecologists want to answer is \\"Why does eusociality exist?\\" It is in attempting to answer this question, within the framework of Darwinian evolutionary theory, that Wilson (and his coauthors Martin Nowak and Corina Tarnita) have ignited another firestorm of controversy in a recent paper entitled \\"The evolution of eusociality\\" that appeared in the pages of the extremely high-profile journal Nature. In this paper and in Wilson's new book, it is posited that eusociality has arisen not from kin-selection based on inclusive fitness, which these authors outrightly discount, but rather the interplay between individual selection and group selection, the one the classic driving force behind Darwinian evolution and the other a largely-abandoned theory that Wilson and his coworkers have resuscitated. Given that the paper in Nature appeared two years ago, there has been time for the Nowak/Tarnita/Wilson explanation to be attacked again and again. In addition, as soon as Wilson's book hit the stands, it was excoriatingly assaulted by the father of the selfish-gene concept, Richard Dawkins. Wilson appears a bit touchy on this subject in his book, since he goes on and on about how his theory of multilevel selection (individual plus group selection)has pretty much trashed the previously prevailing theory. As Wilson notes, the battle between these two competing theories will be waged continuingly in the years to come. For many readers, however, I among them, the most important of the three fundamental questions asked in Gauguin's painting and in Wilson's book is the third one, \\"Where are we going?\\" After all, it is by study of the past that we will find satisfyingly complete answers to the first two questions and since the past isn't going anywhere, we presumably have time to devise the answers. The third question, however, is one for which we can seemingly shape the answer. Even though E. O. Wilson is unquestioningly my greatest biologist hero, I am somewhat ashamed to say that his attempt at an answer is at best tantalizingly brief and at worst a waste of time, his to create it and mine to read it. I have to admit that I often have found his prescriptions of humanity's future to fall short, almost as though he fears to have to say the hard words. I found this to be the case in one of his seminal works The Future of Life (2002), which he ended with the following: \\"A civilization able to envision God and to embark on the colonization of space will surely find the way to save the integrity of this planet and the magnificent life it harbors.\\" In other words, if we can do the one, we ought to be able to do the other. I don't buy it. Wilson's statement appears to be a non sequitur. In the current book, he does much the same thing. In the last paragraph of an 11-page chapter entitled \\"A New Enlightenment,\\" he concludes with the following: \\"So, now I will confess my own blind faith. Earth, by the twenty-second century, can be turned, if we so wish, into a permanent paradise for human beings, or at least the strong beginnings of one. We will do a lot more damage to ourselves and the rest of life along the way, but out of an ethic of simple decency to one another, the unrelenting application of reason, and acceptance of what we truly are (emphasis mine), our dreams will finally come home to stay.\\" I am glad that Wilson ascribes this solution to the sixth great extinction crisis to \\"blind faith,\\" because since faith is defined, in one sense, as \\"belief that does not rest on logical proof or material evidence,\\" then I would have to say that Wilson's hope does not have a secure evidentiary foundation. In fact, a fair response to his closing paragraph would require another book that really does need to be written. So, if I think only two of his central questions have been answered, then what kind of \\"grade\\" do I give to a student who answered only two out of the three questions on the test? That looks like 67% or a D. On a five-point scale, as used at amazon.com, that would be a 3.4 rounded up to the nearest tenth or a 3 rounded down to the nearest whole integer. Okay, I am going to bite the bullet and give him a 3.

What a shamelss ripoff of writing!  And from a production backed by Robert Redford?!?  How shameful.  The movie just dropped at least one star. Even the times when the court adjourns were similar.  I have never seen such a flagrantcopy of a movie's rythm, scenes, dialogue and even shots.  Maybe this happens a lot more than I know, but I'm just shocked that the production designers, writers, director or whoever was responsible for this did not even make an attempt to hide it.  It's remarkable and so very disappointing coming from Robert Redford.  I have such great respect for his lifelong body of work, but this movie has truly changed my perspective.  If he can't make movies any more without copying from someone else (or allowing his staff to do the copying), then perhaps it's time for him to hang it up.  On the other hand, Breaker Morant is a truly excellent army-themed courtroom drama, also based on real events.  It was made in the early 1980's but the production stands very strong even today.  Rent and watch that if you want to see this genre done well. In the end, because of this plageurism-like copying, I had to give the movie 2 stars.  Oh, and Evan Rachel Wood acted terribly, Kevin Kline over-acted his role, McAvoy's wife was just wooden and terrible and many of the other characters were nothing more than characatures.  Even without the copying, this movie is not deserving of more than 3 stars.  The costumes and sets were good though, as was a lot of the cinematography. B001U0HBPG|4.0|The Curious Case of Benjamin Button is based on a story by F. Scott Fitzgerald about a man, born in 1918, who ages in reverse.  Benjamin's baby body looks like an 86-year-old with the same illnesses common to that age.  As the years pass, the body becomes younger.  The film explores the conundrums of this condition, such as  falling in love and fathering a child.  Brad Pitt plays Benjamin simply and admirably, assisted by technology to give him an aged body and by younger actors in closing scenes.  Cate Blanchett plays Daisy, a dancer (she does almost all of her own dancing).  Their love relationship begins when both are adolescents; yet he appears to be an old codger, upsetting Daisy's grandmother.  They are perfectly matched when they are in their forties.  But then a child is born and decisions must be made, as they know Benjamin will become gradually younger.  The story is told in a nursing home in New Orleans in 2005.  Hurricane Katrina is raging as a dying Daisy has her daughter read Benjamin's diary to her.  We see the flashbacks of his jaunt through the 20th century, and Daisy's daughter discovers her identity.  The film evokes contemplation on that century and on the nature of time itself.

0805095535|5.0|Paul Auster wrote an autobiographical book - \\"The Winter Journal\\". As a faithful fan of Auster's prose, I had to read it - and I loved it. He begins with his childhood, and recalling the earliest events he can remember, at the same time philosophizes about memories, their accuracy, exploring the connection of the body, intellect and emotions. He writes about his scars, places where he lived, his love for his wife (the description of the car accident is so sincere!). He tries to come to terms with his age, getting old, his mistakes, his phobias and his weaknesses.<br />Auster is a brilliant writer. And yes, all the other reviewers have critiqued is correct - there are lists, collections of notes, loose bits. But everything is written in the unique Auster style and I cannot help loving this book like all his other books. I wonder how much of it is the literary creation, because I am aware it must be, but it seems very honest. It is really a journal, meaning that different thoughts are noted as the come, there is no chronology, and yet it reads wonderfully and the prose flows without stumbling.

B008N5UX9M|2.0|Anyone who is as big of a Maiden fan as I am would naturally be excited upon hearing that Steve Harris is releasing a solo album.  However, before listening to a single note and researching how it all came to be, it becomes immediately clear that Steve Harris has a very different definition of 'solo album' than, well, anybody else.<br /><br />Steve has spoken of the already established band \\"British Lion\\" as early as 1992.  Look for the interview on the Maidenfans site.  It's a band he discovered and was looking to help.  Now, 20 years later, the band's debut album has finally seen the light of day.  Yes, Steve Harris has joined the band, and supposedly co-wrote all the songs (but as in Maiden, this could simply mean he helped with arrangements and/or perhaps added a little section to a pre-existing song, etc).  I have no doubt the plan is to use Steve's name to get the album exposure.  But make no mistake; this is by no means a true solo album.<br /><br />All that aside, if it's good, it's good.  Unfortunately, it really isn't.  Much has been made about the poor vocals on this album.  Really, they are indeed pretty thin, lack power, and have a certain amateurish quality.  Additionally, the production of the album is pretty weak.  The guitars and drums are underneath the bass (even much more so than on recent Maiden albums), and the album has a demo sounding quality to it.  But most importantly, I find the songs rather dull.  I can honestly say I find none of it very catchy, and it's mostly generic rock that never gets too adventurous.<br /><br />I am just glad we live in an age where music can be streamed legally before deciding to purchase it.  Wanting to give the CD a fair chance, I have listened to it about 5 times, and I do believe I have reached my limit.  I just hope Steve doesn't waste too much time on this project, at least not to the point where Maiden is put on the back burner.  I'm sure it's fun for Steve to do something away from Maiden, though, and I do respect him doing something different, even if it's something I can't get into on any level.  And heck, if worse comes to worse and Maiden does go on hiatus, maybe it would mean that it allows time for Bruce Dickinson to make another solo record.  If that becomes the case, then I guess I would have some reason to be thankful for British Lion.
